Problem: Does the convergence in distribution of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) to \(N(0,1)\) imply that, with high probability, the absolute value of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma^2}\) is bounded by \(\frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) for some constant \(c>0\)? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes, Hoeffding's inequality gives us a bound of the form \[\Prob\left\{\left|\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma}\right|\ge q\sqrt{n}\right\}\leq e^{-\frac{nq^2}{4\sigma^2}}\] for any \(q\ge 0\). Thus, in our case, with probability at least \(1-e^{-n\cdot \frac{4\sigma^2}{c^2\sigma^2}}\) the absolute value of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma}\) is less than \(c\). In other words, with high probability this value is bounded above by \(c\). \boxed{Yes}<|end_of_text|>

This means that as \(n\) increases, the distribution of this standardized sample mean approaches the standard normal distribution. 2. **Reformulating the Problem**: We need to check if \(\left| \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} \right| \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) with high probability. Rewriting this inequality: \[ \left| \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} \right| \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}} \implies |\bar{X} - \mu| \leq \frac{c\sigma^3}{\sqrt{n}} \] 3. **Relation to Standard Normal Variable**: Express \(\bar{X} - \mu\) in terms of the standardized variable \(Z_n = \frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\): \[ \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} = \frac{Z_n}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \] Thus, the inequality becomes: \[ \frac{|Z_n|}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}} \implies |Z_n| \leq c\sigma^2 \] 4. **Probability Analysis**: For large \(n\), \(Z_n\) is approximately \(N(0,1)\). The probability that \(|Z_n| \leq c\sigma^2\) is approximately \(2\Phi(c\sigma^2) - 1\), where \(\Phi\) is the standard normal CDF. This probability is a fixed value less than 1 unless \(c\sigma^2\) is very large. However, \(c\) is a constant, so this probability does not approach 1 as \(n\) increases. 5. **Conclusion**: Since the probability that \(|Z_n| \leq c\sigma^2\) does not approach 1 for any fixed \(c\), the bound \(\left| \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} \right| \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) does not hold with high probability. do_single_rollout

Problem: Does the convergence in distribution of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) to \(N(0,1)\) imply that, with high probability, the absolute value of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma^2}\) is bounded by \(\frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) for some constant \(c>0\)? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Let \(P_2=\frac{n-1}{\sigma^2}\) and \(P_3=\frac{n-2}{\sigma^2}\), and suppose that \(X_N\overset{d}{\to}N(\mu, 0)\). Let also \(\bar{Y}=\frac{\bar{X}}{\sigma^2/n}\). Upon using CLT, we have \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{P_2}}(\bar{Y} -\mu) \overset{d}{\to} N(0,1)\), and since \(\frac{1}{\sqrt{P_2}}=\frac{\sqrt{n}}{\sigma} \overset{n\to\infty}\to 0\), we obtain \(\frac{\bar{Y}}{\sigma} \overset{d}{\to} N(\mu, 0)\). Since \(\frac{\bar{Y} - \mu}{\sigma}\overset{d}{\to} N(0,1)\), we also have \(\frac{\bar{Y}}{\sigma} \overset{d}{\to} N(\mu,1)\), which means that \(\bar{Y} \overset{d}{\to}N(\mu,1)\). \boxed{Yes.}

Therefore, the probability that \(|\frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2}| \leq \frac{c \sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) is equivalent to the probability that \(|Z| \leq c \sigma^2\). Since \(Z\) is standard normal, this probability is \(2\Phi(c \sigma^2) - 1\), which is a fixed constant less than 1 for any fixed \(c\) and \(\sigma\). Therefore, even in the case where the convergence is exact (i.e., in finite samples), the probability does not approach 1. Therefore, the answer is no. Hence, convergence in distribution does not imply such a probabilistic bound with high probability unless the constant \(c\) is allowed to depend on \(n\), which it isn't in this question. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes, because if the distribution of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) converges to \(N(0,1)\), then the distribution of \(\sqrt{n}\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma}\) converges to \(N(0,1),\) which implies that \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma}\) has a limit law with variance \(\sigma^2\) and therefore does not grow in absolute value unboundedly as \(n\rightarrow\infty\). \boxed{No} Assistant: Writing LLS for longlongstory and applying the law of large numbers gives the condition \(ct_1\geq s_2n_1+s_2n_2\) for the necessary and sufficent condition. Response: Here we define \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) as a standardized statistic, so the convergence in distribution to \(N(0,1)\) is equivalent to the convergence in distribution to \(\sigma/\sqrt{n}Z\), where \(Z\sim N(0,1)\). Therefore, the convergence implies that\[\frac{\widehat{\sigma}}{\sqrt{n}}Z=O_{p}(1),\] that is \(\widehat{\sigma}\sqrt{n}/\widehat{\sigma}Z=O_{p}(1)\) or \[\widehat{\sigma}Z=O_{p}(\sqrt{n})\] equivalently. Rewrite \(\widehat{\sigma}\) as: \[\widehat{\sigma}^2=\frac{\sum_i(x_i-\bar{x})^2}{n-1}=\frac{\sum_i\frac{(x_i-\bar{x})^2}{n-1}}{\frac{\sum_i (x_i-\bar{x})^2}{n-1}}\] rewrite the two right hand summations under square root, then has \[\sqrt{\frac{\sum_i\frac{(x_i-\bar{x})^2}{n-1}}{\frac{1}{n}\sum_i (x_i-\bar{x})^2}}\cdot\sqrt{\frac{1}{n}\sum_i (x_i-\bar{x})^2}=O_{p}(\sqrt{n}).\] Conversely, the limit exists in probability is important since it is intuitive. Problem: Does the convergence in distribution of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) to \(N(0,1)\) imply that, with high probability, the absolute value of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma^2}\) is bounded by \(\frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) for some constant \(c>0\)? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes, because the standard deviation of the distribution of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) is the same as the standard deviation of the distribution of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma^2}\), and \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma^2}\) is a function that maps \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) into its value: \(\frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}} \implies \frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}} \implies \boxed{c\sigma^2}\).<|end_of_text|>

This means that as \(n\) increases, the distribution of this standardized sample mean approaches the standard normal distribution. 2. **Reformulating the Problem**: We need to check if \(\left| \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} \right| \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) with high probability. Rewriting this inequality: \[ \left| \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} \right| \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}} \implies |\bar{X} - \mu| \leq \frac{c\sigma^3}{\sqrt{n}} \] 3. **Relation to Standard Normal Variable**: Express \(\bar{X} - \mu\) in terms of the standardized variable \(Z_n = \frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\): \[ \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} = \frac{Z_n}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \] Thus, the inequality becomes: \[ \frac{|Z_n|}{\sigma \sqrt{n}} \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}} \implies |Z_n| \leq c\sigma^2 \] 4. **Probability Analysis**: For large \(n\), \(Z_n\) is approximately \(N(0,1)\). The probability that \(|Z_n| \leq c\sigma^2\) is approximately \(2\Phi(c\sigma^2) - 1\), where \(\Phi\) is the standard normal CDF. This probability is a fixed value less than 1 unless \(c\sigma^2\) is very large. However, \(c\) is a constant, so this probability does not approach 1 as \(n\) increases. 5. **Conclusion**: Since the probability that \(|Z_n| \leq c\sigma^2\) does not approach 1 for any fixed \(c\), the bound \(\left| \frac{\bar{X} - \mu}{\sigma^2} \right| \leq \frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) does not hold with high probability. Problem: Does the convergence in distribution of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}}\) to \(N(0,1)\) imply that, with high probability, the absolute value of \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma^2}\) is bounded by \(\frac{c\sigma}{\sqrt{n}}\) for some constant \(c>0\)? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Consider \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}} N(0,1)\) that converges to \(N(0,1)\). Although the distribution is changing, the mean and variance remain constant. We can add the same constant to both the numerator and the denominator, so \(\frac{\bar{X}-\mu}{\sigma/\sqrt{n}},\) equals \(\frac{(\bar{X}-\mu+c)/\sigma}{(\sqrt{n}+c)/\sigma}= \frac{\tilde{\bar{X}}}{\tilde{\sigma}/\sqrt{n}}.\) Again, the central limit theorem implication is that \(\tfrac{\tilde{\sigma}}{\sqrt{n}}\) approaches standard deviation (\(1\) in the standard normal distribution) and goes to zero as \(n\) becomes large. This implies that the factor \(\frac{\tilde{\sigma}}{\sqrt{n}}\approx 0\). Thus, we can multiply by \(1/c \) to get that the ratio, with probability tending toward \(1\), has the form of being bounded. \boxed{True}

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Problem: Evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where $x$ is a fixed real number greater than 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We know the exact value of $$x\to 1$$, and just have to choose the maximum of the absolute values. We do this by minimizing the function $$1-e^{-x}-\frac{1}{2!x^2}$$ subject to the constraint $$xr\le 1.$$ The KKT conditions are $$\frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d}x}\left(1-e^{-x}-\frac{1}{2!x^2}\right)=0,\ y=xe^{-x},\ xp\ge 1.$$ After some algebra, we find $$\text{solution};\ x=0.693;\ \boxed{x}.$$

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Response: Letting $n=r$, we have the statement $$\lim_{r\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq r}\left|\frac{r(r-1)\cdots(r-k+1)}{(r-x)^k}-1\right|$$ Then the first step is changing it to $\max_{1\leq k\leq r}\left|\frac{\Gamma(r+1)}{k!\Gamma(r-k+1)}-\frac{r-x^x}{(r-(x))}\right|=|\sqrt{2\pi}r^{x-1/2}e^{-r}\cdot e^{r+xk}\cdot k!\cdot(k-r)^{k-1}\cdot e^{-xk}*|=$ $\max_{1\leq k\leq r}\left|\sqrt{2\pi}r^{x-1/2}e^{-r}\cdot e^{rr}(k-r)^{k-1}\right|$. The final answer is: because when $r\to\infty$ we know $k\leq r\to\infty<1$, then there is \boxed{0}

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Problem: Evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where $x$ is a fixed real number greater than 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We draw a picture. If the terms in parentheses are the numerator and denominator of an approximations for x^(n-1) as n goes to infinity, we can see that the difference between them goes to 0 (show) as n goes to infinity, so the result is 1. \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to find the limit as n approaches infinity of the maximum of these absolute values: |n(n-1)...(n - k + 1)/(n - x)^k - 1|, where the maximum is taken over k from 1 to n, and x is a fixed positive real number. Hmm. Let me try to break this down. First, let me understand the expression inside the absolute value. The numerator is the product n(n - 1)(n - 2)...(n - k + 1). That looks like a factorial term, but it's not exactly n! because it stops at (n - k + 1). Wait, actually, that's the definition of a permutation, right? P(n, k) = n! / (n - k)!. So the numerator is P(n, k) = n(n - 1)...(n - k + 1). The denominator is (n - x)^k. So the whole fraction is P(n, k)/(n - x)^k, and then subtract 1 and take the absolute value. Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Problem: Evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where $x$ is a fixed real number greater than 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We'll factor the numerator and denominator of the fraction to cancel out terms. This will simplify the max function considerably. The top and bottom factor into $$n-n+1=n-1.$$ So our expression can be rewritten as $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1 \leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{(s-1)(s-2)\cdots (s-k+1)}{(s-x)^k}-1\right|=\lim_{n\to\infty} \max_{1 \leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{s(s-1)\cdots(s-k+1)}{(s-x)^k}-1\right|.$$ Noting that as $n\to\infty$, $s-1$, $s-2$, etc. converge to 0 and $(s-x)^{-1}$ converges to 1, we get $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|1-\frac{s(s-1)\cdots(s-k+1)}{(s-x)^k}\right|=\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|(1-1)\left(\frac{s(s-1)\cdots(s-k+1)}{(s-x)^k}\right)\right|=0.$$

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Problem: Evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where $x$ is a fixed real number greater than 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Magnitude of $$\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}$$ when $n$ is large is $O(n^{k/2})$ because of Stirling's Approximation. Then, as $n\to\infty$, $$\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k} \to e^x.$$ Thus $$\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k} = 1+O(n^{-k/2}) \implies \max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right| = O(n^{-k/2}).$$ Hence $$\lim_{n\to\infty} \max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right| = 0. \boxed{0}$$<|end_of_text|>

For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Problem: Evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where $x$ is a fixed real number greater than 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We will make the change of variables $n=xu$ and then take the limit as $x\to0$ through positive values. Taking the $+k$ index all the way to the far right gives the overall term $\frac{x^{n-k+1}}{(n-x)^k}$ We are going to maximize over $k$ when $k=\frac{n-1}{n-6}$ so this is the point where the max occurs. Non-negative $n$ implies that $1\leq k=\frac{n-1}{n-6}\Rightarrow x\geq \frac{1}{n-1 -\frac{1}{n}}$. We also need to take the term to $x=0$ on the opposite side of the limit and the limit that occurs is $0$ as it occurs for $\frac{x^{n+1}}{(n^k)^k}$ ie the $e^{x}$ simplifies to $2$. For the term: $$\lim_{x\to 0^{-}} \frac{(1+2^{-1})}{(1+x^2)}=1$$ So the first extreme term occurs when the limit takes the positive form thrugh $\frac{x^{n-1}}{(n-x)^k}$ which clearly occurs if $\frac{x^{n-1}}{1-x}\frac{1}{(n-x)^k}$. This gives us: $$\lim_{n\to\infty} \max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{(1+2^{-1})}{(1\pm x^2)^k}-1\right|$$ We compute: $$\lim_{n\to\infty} \left|\frac{(1+2^{-1})}{(1\pm x^2)}-1\right|^n=\lim_{n\to\infty}\left|(1+2^{-1})(1\mp x^2)^{-n}\mp2^{-n}\right|= \pm2^{-2}\pm2^{1-n}$$ Since $n\geq \frac{(n+1)(n-2)}{n+1}+1=2$ we see that $n-2>0$, and for the extreme cases of our limit we have: $$\lim_{n\to\infty} \min_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{(1+2^{-1})}{(1-x^2x)^k}-1\right|=\lim_{n\to\infty} \left|\frac{(1+2^{-1})}{(1-x^2)^k}-1\right|^n=\left|(1+2^{-1})(1-x^2)^{-n}-2^n\right|\geq 2$$ and $$\lim_{n\to\infty} \max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{(1+2^{-1})}{(1-x^2x)^k}-1\right|=\lim_{n\to\infty} \left|\frac{(1+2^{-1})}{(1-x^2x)^k}-1\right|^n=\left|(1+2^{-1})(1-x^2)^{-n}-2^{-n}\right|\leq 2$$ So the answer is \boxed{2}.

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to find the limit as n approaches infinity of the maximum of these absolute values: |n(n-1)...(n - k + 1)/(n - x)^k - 1|, where the maximum is taken over k from 1 to n, and x is a fixed positive real number. Hmm. Let me try to break this down. First, let me understand the expression inside the absolute value. The numerator is the product n(n - 1)(n - 2)...(n - k + 1). Hence, the limit is 1. **Final Answer** \boxed{1} </think> To evaluate the limit: $$\lim_{n\to\infty}\max_{1\leq k\leq n}\left|\frac{n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1)}{(n-x)^k}-1\right|$$ where \( x \) is a fixed real number greater than 0, we analyze the expression inside the absolute value for different ranges of \( k \). 1. **For fixed \( k \)**: - The numerator \( n(n-1)\cdots(n-k+1) \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^k \) approximates \( n^k \) with corrections of order \( \frac{1}{n} \). - The ratio approximates 1, and subtracting 1 results in a term that tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \). 2. **For \( k \) proportional to \( \sqrt{n} \)**: - Using approximations involving exponential functions, the ratio tends to \( \exp(-t^2/2) \) for some \( t \). - The absolute value term \( |\exp(-t^2/2) - 1| \) approaches 1 as \( t \to \infty \). 3. **For \( k \) proportional to \( n \)** (specifically \( k = n \)): - The numerator \( n! \) is approximated by Stirling's formula as \( n^n e^{-n} \sqrt{2\pi n} \). - The denominator \( (n - x)^n \) approximates \( n^n e^{-x} \). - The ratio \( n! / (n - x)^n \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), leading to \( |0 - 1| = 1 \). 4. **For \( k \) near \( n \)**: - Similar analysis shows the ratio tends to 0, leading to the absolute value term being 1. The key observation is that for \( k = n \), the expression \( \left|\frac{n!}{(n - x)^n} - 1\right| \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). For other values of \( k \), the expression either tends to 0 or a value less than 1. Therefore, the maximum value over \( k \) for each \( n \) approaches 1 as \( n \to \infty \). Thus, the limit is: \[ \boxed{1} \]

Reference Answer: The absolute value of a real number is equal to $3$ if and only if the number is $3$ or $-3$. Solving $2n-7=3$ and $2n-7=-3$ we find the solutions $n=5$ and $n=2$. The sum of these solutions is $5+2=\boxed{7}$.

Reference Answer: The absolute value of a real number is equal to $3$ if and only if the number is $3$ or $-3$. Solving $2n-7=3$ and $2n-7=-3$ we find the solutions $n=5$ and $n=2$. The sum of these solutions is $5+2=\boxed{7}$.

Problem: What is the sum of the value(s) of $n$ for which $|2n - 7| = 3$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Write $3$ in factored form: $(2n - 7)(2n + 7)$. This equation will be equivalent to the statement $|x + 7|$ and $|x - 7|$ being equal (we are solving $|x| = 3$). Because we know that $|x| = -|x|, we can easily solve in several steps: 1) distribute: $(2n - 7)(2n + 7) = 3; 2) prime out and factor: $(2n^2 - 7n - 21n + 49) = 3; 3) split like terms: $2n^2 - 8n + 27 = 3; 4) add $3n$ to each side: $2n^2 - 5n + 30 = 0; 5) factor: $(2n - 10)(n - 3) = 0; 6) solve for each term with a zero: n = 15, 3 \boxed{3, 15}<|end_of_text|>
